  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5eae7fde-d477-4d0f-850e-cecf3b37d47b'
      });

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1291393306i/1598886._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp">Ian Mapp</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Not really, they are all standalone stories, with some character's re-appearing. If you do read them in order, you have a choice. The back of the book<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Not really, they are all standalone stories, with some character's re-appearing. If you do read them in order, you have a choice. The back of the books lists them in historical order (which is the way I am reading them) or in published order. This particular book is interesting, as it was published before (1988) the first 5 in historical order (published between 1997-2001). Hope that helps. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_3094668280" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3094668280" />
    <a title="Alex Cantone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48050946-alex-cantone"><img alt="Alex Cantone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479001219p2/48050946.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3094668280?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Cantone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Cantone" href="/user/show/48050946-alex-cantone">Alex Cantone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007870745" href="/book/show/36600847-sharpe-s-rifles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48050946-alex-cantone?shelf=read-2019">read-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48050946-alex-cantone?shelf=military">military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48050946-alex-cantone?shelf=historic-fiction">historic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3094668280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11809608225872979292"><i>Sharpe knew himself to be a tough man, but he had always thought of himself as a reasonable one, yet now, in the mirror of William’s nervousness, he saw himself as something far worse: a bullying man who would use the small authority of his rank to frighten men…</i><br/><br/>In February 1809 the British army under Sir John Moore is routed by Napoleon’s forces and is in retreat to the port of La Coruna in North-west Spain’s Galicia region, hoping to escape in Dunkirk-fashion back to England. Lieutenant Richard</span>
  <span id="freeText11809608225872979292" style="display:none"><i>Sharpe knew himself to be a tough man, but he had always thought of himself as a reasonable one, yet now, in the mirror of William’s nervousness, he saw himself as something far worse: a bullying man who would use the small authority of his rank to frighten men…</i><br /><br />In February 1809 the British army under Sir John Moore is routed by Napoleon’s forces and is in retreat to the port of La Coruna in North-west Spain’s Galicia region, hoping to escape in Dunkirk-fashion back to England. Lieutenant Richard Sharpe and the men of the 95th Rifles under his command become separated from the bulk of the troops, and are trapped at a river crossing, saved by Spanish <i>Cazadores</i>, led by the charismatic aristocrat Don Blas Vivar. <br /><br />Sharpe wants to head south to the border with Portugal to join the British garrison at Lisbon, a decision at odds with his men who distrust an officer risen from the ranks, and he is equally despised by officers of allied armies and the enemy alike. Vivar enlists them to first head west to accompany his men carrying a strongbox which, the Riflemen believe, contains gold and jewels to prevent it falling into the hands of the enemy. Progress is slow and arduous, avoiding the roads, and they encounter a gutted village where they see at first hand the savagery of the French against the peasants.<br /><br /><i>They journeyed through the night, climbing ever higher and always into the teeth of a wind that brought the chill from the snow which lay in the gullies of the upper slopes. Past midnight, from a wooded spur, Sharpe saw the far off gleam of the western sea. Much closer, and beneath him in the dark tangle of the lowlands, a smear of camp fires betrayed where men were bivouacked. ‘The French,’ Vivar said softly.</i><br /><br />Vivar, a passionately religious man, rails against Sharpe (an agnostic), but then they are side-tracked by an English couple and their niece, dour Methodist missionaries, there to convert the Spanish away from Papism, with little success.<br /><br />The French are persistent and Sharpe realises that they are after the coveted strongbox they have been duped to protect. Finally, in a high country fortress disused for centuries, Vivar reveals its contents and their destination as the city of <i>Santiago</i> (St James – the patron saint of Spain) <i>de Compostello</i> (field of stars), which he is to use as a rallying point, invoking the spirit of St James to help the Spanish rid the country of the French, as he had nine centuries earlier against the Moors. Naturally, some Spaniards disagree, seeing the French as “enlightened and progressive”.<br /><br />This is a novel on many levels, binding history with fiction. At its centre, a brilliant soldier and tactician, leadership thrust upon him yet with barely the skills to lead, who needs to gain the respect of his men. The reader follows his progress, from self-doubt to victory in battle (and there are several of those). Secondly, there is the attention to detail, of weaponry and uniform - the Riflesmen’s tattered rags and boot soles wound with twine, contrasting with the finery of the enemy. We learn what they eat (bare rations supplemented from what they glean from the land or are given by villagers). Then there are the smells of warfare: blood, vomit, horses chaffed and ridden almost to death and taking the brunt of the attack (not recommended for animal lovers). Finally there is the location itself, <i>Santiago de Compostello</i>, the destination of pilgrim tracks from across Europe for 1200 years, now UNESCO-listed and visited by millions. <br /><br />Verdict: a read to be savoured, not rushed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11809608225872979292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_557788681" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/557788681" />

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio 9780786179930" href="/book/show/3947721-sharpe-s-rifles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=3series">3series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer557788681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1654493292397520646">More of a 2.5, but definitely my least favorite of the series so far. I didn't care for the reader, Fredrick Davidson, which didn't help. While he was tolerable, his Spanish voices were very low, gravelly, & almost unintelligible at times, while all his Irish voices were very high & lilting. Since one of the Irishmen was a really big, tough sergeant, the high voice didn't fit my image of a red haired Lee Marvin well.<br/><br/>Sharpe wasn't shown in a very good light for most of the book & the historical a</span>
  <span id="freeText1654493292397520646" style="display:none">More of a 2.5, but definitely my least favorite of the series so far. I didn't care for the reader, Fredrick Davidson, which didn't help. While he was tolerable, his Spanish voices were very low, gravelly, &amp; almost unintelligible at times, while all his Irish voices were very high &amp; lilting. Since one of the Irishmen was a really big, tough sergeant, the high voice didn't fit my image of a red haired Lee Marvin well.<br /><br />Sharpe wasn't shown in a very good light for most of the book &amp; the historical afterword basically said that "it didn't happen that way". One of the things that I like about the Sharpe books is that they usually DID happen that way, with a few minor changes to put Sharpe into the action. The sinking feeling in my stomach when I heard the news told me just how much I like that &amp; have come to rely on Cornwall's historical accuracy. It's kind of the point for me.<br /><br />There were quite a few editorial goofs, too. For example, an officer was herding his men by hitting them with the <b>edge</b> of his saber. It was pretty obvious from the context &amp; the results that it was the <b>flat</b>. Caltrops don't just stick into the soft frog of a horse's hoof, but also into the sole, yet several times it was put that way. Irritating. <br /><br />I listened to book #3, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/157180.Sharpe_s_Fortress__Sharpe___3_" title="Sharpe's Fortress (Sharpe, #3) by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Fortress</a>, just before this. My library doesn't have books #4 &amp; #5 in audio format, but I read the 5th, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/492919.Sharpe_s_Prey__Sharpe___5_" title="Sharpe's Prey (Sharpe, #5) by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Prey</a> some years ago. The next audio book I have is #9, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/108386.Sharpe_s_Gold__Sharpe___9_" title="Sharpe's Gold (Sharpe, #9) by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Gold</a>. I think I'll take a break for a while &amp; maybe come up with book 7 or 8 in the meantime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1654493292397520646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_65045023" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65045023" />
    <a title="Kate Sherrod" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1854297-kate-sherrod"><img alt="Kate Sherrod" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442598495p2/1854297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65045023?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate Sherrod" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate Sherrod" href="/user/show/1854297-kate-sherrod">Kate Sherrod</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65045023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8515624884386536121">"He might not be a born officer, but by God he was a born soldier. He was the son of a whore, bereft of God, but a God-damned soldier."<br/><br/>I've decided that the best way to approach the Sharpe series -- in which the publication order differs so radically from the publication order as to seem all but an exercise in randomization -- the way one does when reading stories about Conan the Cimmerian. There might be some narrative carry-over from novel to novel, but it's best to just regard them as discret</span>
  <span id="freeText8515624884386536121" style="display:none">"He might not be a born officer, but by God he was a born soldier. He was the son of a whore, bereft of God, but a God-damned soldier."<br /><br />I've decided that the best way to approach the Sharpe series -- in which the publication order differs so radically from the publication order as to seem all but an exercise in randomization -- the way one does when reading stories about Conan the Cimmerian. There might be some narrative carry-over from novel to novel, but it's best to just regard them as discrete stories that happen to be about a guy with the same name and more or less the same character.<br /><br />I say this because Sharpe's Rifles is the point where a lot of people who have chosen to read these books in chronological order start complaining about inconsistencies. The book was written some half a dozen years after those of the original core series, but cast as a prequel to them -- and the books I've read so far were written many, many years after this one, but take place earlier in Sharpe's career.<br /><br />So in a lot of ways, the Richard Sharpe in Sharpe's Rifles bears little resemblance to the character I've grown to love through his adventures in India, at sea, and in Denmark, except in the ways described in the ur-Cornwellian sentence I quoted at the beginning of this post. He's still pretty uncouth and brutal, still an all but conscience-less and cold-blooded killer, but he seems only to have honed those qualities from his prior adventures* but not to have experienced the character building that came with them. To wit: he is unsure in his authority (though it could be argued that the years he has spent as a downtrodden Quartermaster for the 95th Rifles might have eroded the confidence he gained in India and Denmark), a complete sucker for anything in a skirt (see my asterisk below) and taking lessons in leadership from the Spanish major Bias Vivar that he really ought already to have absorbed from the good examples of his protectors in India like McCandless.<br /><br />But these are small quibbles, and become meaningless once one has agreed to treat the novels as things outside of time and narrative continuity. Especially when the material at hand is so good, as it is here. For Sharpe's Rifles has everything I've come to expect from a Sharpe story: over-the-top adventure (here a ragtag band of survivors of a famous retreat across Spain is teaming up with a small-but-elite cadre of the Spanish army commanded by the aforementioned Don Bias on a mission to bring a Holy McGuffin to the Cathedral at Santiago de Compostella and thus create a new legend to inspire the Spanish peasantry to rise up against the hated French invaders), internecine bickering, inspired combat tactics, cold chivalry among enemies, and all the fighting, drinking and swearing (if not, this time, the whoring) one might expect from a good piece of military fiction.<br /><br />Here, too, is an origin story of sorts, though its significance is lost to chronological readers who have not osmotically absorbed a certain level of meta-knowledge about the series -- for it is here that Sharpe and his gonna-be best friend, Sergeant Harper, meet for the first time. And it's a pretty good meet as those go -- Harper almost stages a mutiny against Sharpe! -- but it's still not as good as Aubrey and Maturin and the concert at Port Mahon. But that's maybe not a fair comparison, right? I'm sure back in the 1980s when only the original core Peninsular War books existed, fans of Sharpe/Harper were delighted to observe this meeting, but for us chronological readers starting in the 21st century, it will never have the same power.<br /><br />Still, cracking good stuff. Again, lots of explorations of how the rifle changed warfare, and how swords still matter, even if one sword is in the hand of a guy astride a big horse and the other in the hand of a guy on foot who ran out of ammo or out of time to reload his weapon, lots of amusing ruses de guerre... and then there's the attack on Santiago itself, which doesn't hold a candle to the big set-piece battles we saw in India, but is still very satisfying indeed.<br /><br />Truly, Sharpe never disappoints.<br /><br />*At least, thank goodness, his prior adventures don't involve a lot of ret-conning; the allusions to his deeds in India, at Seringapatam and Gawalghur, etc. match up with the stories I've read. Well, except for Lady Grace, his lover from Sharpe's Waterloo who died after giving him a son before Sharpe's Prey. I'm pretty sure that once you've bedded a gorgeous noblewoman you're not going to be so terribly overawed by a mere member of the impecunious country gentry, however mischievous and cute.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8515624884386536121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38425171" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38425171" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38425171?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38425171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16342211834095074995">If you're not going to start at the beginning, this is a great place to start Bernard Cornwell's long-running Napoleonic War series. The events in <i>Sharpe's Rifles</i> take place when European hostilities between the French and English really took off, and it also contains the initial meeting between our hero Sharpe and his trusty sidekick Harper. Besides that, it's just a solid book in this truly action-packed series. <br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2690070083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2690070083" />
    <a title="Carol Storm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5032322-carol-storm"><img alt="Carol Storm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442507712p2/5032322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2690070083?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Storm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Storm" href="/user/show/5032322-carol-storm">Carol Storm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2690070083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5472474695880081607">This read almost like an origins story for Sharpe. It shows how he first took command of the Rifles, how Sergeant Harper because his best friend, and how the British army began the long process of driving Napoleon and the French out of Spain. <br/><br/>The best thing about this book by far was actually nothing to do with Sharpe -- it was the way the author describes the uprising against the French. The way the Spanish peasants ambush and savagely assault the regular French troops has a disturbingly modern</span>
  <span id="freeText5472474695880081607" style="display:none">This read almost like an origins story for Sharpe. It shows how he first took command of the Rifles, how Sergeant Harper because his best friend, and how the British army began the long process of driving Napoleon and the French out of Spain. <br /><br />The best thing about this book by far was actually nothing to do with Sharpe -- it was the way the author describes the uprising against the French. The way the Spanish peasants ambush and savagely assault the regular French troops has a disturbingly modern feel. Sharpe is actually more of a war correspondent than a soldier in a lot of this, as he makes you feel like you are right there and witnessing history. It's amazing when you compare this book to so called literary works like HOMAGE TO CATALONIA by George Orwell or THERE YOUR HEART LIES by Mary Gordon. Those books are bout the Spanish Civil War but Spain never comes to life when Mary Gordon or George Orwell write about it. They really don't like the Spanish people or care about them. Bernard Cornwell makes you understand why Spain is so unique and why the people resisted Napoleon. <br /><br />On the other hand, all the writing about Sharpe and Louisa and Don Blas is unbelievably bad. Bernard Cornwell knows nothing about women, nothing about love, and nothing about how people interact with each other on even the most basic levels. It was like wincing on every page to see him try to sell the idea that Sharpe even likes Louisa, let alone that he loves her. And of course he shows the girl as being two-faced, flighty, selfish, and childish all the time he's pretending that Sharpe likes her. It's so bizarre, like Cornwell thinks he has to put a desirable young woman in the book just to maintain the fiction that Sharpe is a "real man." Yet he never seems to relax when women are around! He's so resentful and defensive all the time. And there was one really grotesque scene in a tavern where Sharpe is groping a Spanish whore in front of Don Blas, just to show off. And it was literally a scene a creepy twelve year old boy would make up for the same reason -- because "that's how men behave." Normally I totally hate feminists who talk about stuff like "rape culture," but thanks to Sharpe I see what they mean. <br /><br />Thanks a lot, Sharpe!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5472474695880081607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2217735549" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2217735549" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57725883-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557030750p2/57725883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2217735549?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/57725883-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57725883-rob?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2217735549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9825569978020287002">Number 6 in the Sharpe series.<br/>This story takes place just after the French defeated the British at the battle of Corunna in 1809.<br/> The British are in disarray trying to get to safety via the coast where the British fleet is waiting to evacuate the defeated army.<br/> The 95th Rifles are separated from the main army and are being harassed by French dragoons. There are constant rearguard skirmishes which are taking a toll on the troops of the 95th. In one skirmish the last, but one, officer is killed. T</span>
  <span id="freeText9825569978020287002" style="display:none">Number 6 in the Sharpe series.<br />This story takes place just after the French defeated the British at the battle of Corunna in 1809.<br /> The British are in disarray trying to get to safety via the coast where the British fleet is waiting to evacuate the defeated army.<br /> The 95th Rifles are separated from the main army and are being harassed by French dragoons. There are constant rearguard skirmishes which are taking a toll on the troops of the 95th. In one skirmish the last, but one, officer is killed. The last officer standing is Lt Richard Sharpe. Up to this point Lt Sharpe was the company quartermaster. Look down on by his fellow officer because he was promoted from the ranks. He is also ignored by the rank and file because he’s nothing but a jumped up soldier, just like themselves. They bitterly resent being told what to do by a pretend officer.<br />If these men are to survive, Sharpe has to gain the respect of these men and soon.<br />Whilst struggling with the bitter cold in the Spanish mountains Sharpe and his men fall in with a group of Spanish soldiers. The Spanish troops are on a mission to plant the flag of Santiago in the French garrisoned town of Santiago de Compostela. Although he feels that this is nothing more than a pipe dream, Sharpe feels he has no choice but to fall in with the Spaniards.<br />As usual, there is a young attractive woman, but for once, she is immune to Richard’s masculine appeal. Falling instead for the dashing commander of the Spanish troops.<br /><br />This, once again, is a fast paced, rollicking adventure. <br /><br />Comes with a 4 star recommendation.<br /> <br /></span>

<div id="review_46129638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46129638" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46129638?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780006176978" href="/book/show/906669.Sharpe_s_Rifles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=napoleonic-era">napoleonic-era</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46129638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9910806239487978914">I am reading the Sharpe books in chronological order and have just reached <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/360562.Sharpe_s_Rifles' title='Sharpe's Rifles by Bernard Cornwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sharpe's Rifles</a>, the first meeting of Lt. Richard Sharpe and his best friend, Sgt. Harper, and I have to admit that the moment doesn't mean all that much to me.<br/><br/>It's crafted to be one of those great moments in fiction, and I suppose it could have been if I had approached Sharpe's Rifles from a different direction. Had I been reading the books in order of publication or even seen the occasional installment of the BBC's Shar</span>
  <span id="freeText9910806239487978914" style="display:none">I am reading the Sharpe books in chronological order and have just reached <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/360562.Sharpe_s_Rifles" title="Sharpe's Rifles by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Rifles</a>, the first meeting of Lt. Richard Sharpe and his best friend, Sgt. Harper, and I have to admit that the moment doesn't mean all that much to me.<br /><br />It's crafted to be one of those great moments in fiction, and I suppose it could have been if I had approached Sharpe's Rifles from a different direction. Had I been reading the books in order of publication or even seen the occasional installment of the BBC's Sharpe movies, I think I would have been delighted to see the meeting of these men. But reading the books in order gives me no stake in their relationship. I have come to appreciate Sharpe, but I don't know Harper at all and just don't care about how they met yet. <br /><br />That could change as the series goes on, but I am still not sure it would make a difference as far as their initial meeting is concerned because, as I said, it is "crafted" to be one of those great moments. And I don't know that the series has what it takes to be a "great" series, let alone be important enough for the meeting of its principles to be of serious interest to the literary world at large. Moreover, the crafting of the meeting feels forced in a way that truly great meetings of characters do not. Aubrey and Maturin meeting (a fine example considering their place in the Napoleonic oeuvre), coming as it does in the opening pages of the first book with no need for a prequel, is an organic growth of the story, and its impact is given a chance to grow over the course of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77430.Master_and_Commander__Book_1_" title="Master and Commander (Book 1) by Patrick O'Brian" rel="nofollow noopener">Master and Commander</a> with a feeling that anything could happen. And none of that is the case with Sharpe and Harper. <br /><br />Regardless, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/360562.Sharpe_s_Rifles" title="Sharpe's Rifles by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Rifles</a> is a decent read, but not one of the best in the series. There are too many inconsistencies with the books that come before, and Sharpe's tendency to fall for every pretty girl above his station (which he has done in ever preceding book, yet this is never mentioned in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/360562.Sharpe_s_Rifles" title="Sharpe's Rifles by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Rifles</a>) is already becoming tedious.<br /><br />The battles are rousing, though, and Cornwell always includes something fun and inventive -- like Sharpe's use of caltrops against the French Cavalry -- and Sharpe's brutal efficiency consistently sates the potential bloodlust of Cornwell's readers.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell" title="Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Bernard Cornwell</a> also make sure there are interesting supporting characters. Despite the weakness of Sharpe's meeting with Harper, the Irish Sergeant is a promising partner for Sharpe, and the fiery Blas Vivar, the Spanish Major trying to work a miracle against the French, succeeds in overshadowing everyone in the story. The only complaint in the supporting cast is that every Frenchman is turning into a Napoleonic era Nazi -- too cruel, too dishonourable, too clever and just a little too evil. It will become downright annoying if it continues for the rest of the books, but for now it is merely noteworthy. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/360562.Sharpe_s_Rifles" title="Sharpe's Rifles by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharpe's Rifles</a> is only worth reading if you're committed to the series, if not you can probably get away with watching this installment on BBC. I have a feeling you won't miss anything of importance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9910806239487978914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_731613767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/731613767" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/731613767?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=napoleonic-wars">napoleonic-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer731613767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17828315772679797309"><i>Sharpe's Rifles</i> starts in 1809 with the Peninsular War in Spain already raging. For those who aren't familiar, the Peninsular War was one of the big conflicts of the Napoleonic Wars, with basically Britain, Spain, and Portugal duking it out with the French on the Iberian Peninsula. When we join our hero, the Spanish armies are destroyed and the British are already outnumbered and on the retreat from the French hordes. Due to some typical snobbish officer dumbassery, Sharpe and a small group of R</span>
  <span id="freeText17828315772679797309" style="display:none"><i>Sharpe's Rifles</i> starts in 1809 with the Peninsular War in Spain already raging. For those who aren't familiar, the Peninsular War was one of the big conflicts of the Napoleonic Wars, with basically Britain, Spain, and Portugal duking it out with the French on the Iberian Peninsula. When we join our hero, the Spanish armies are destroyed and the British are already outnumbered and on the retreat from the French hordes. Due to some typical snobbish officer dumbassery, Sharpe and a small group of Riflemen are cut off after an attack on the British rearguard by French cavalry, and must ally with a charismatic Spanish major with his own dangerous mission.<br /><br />Chronologically speaking, this is a new kind of story in the series; it's one where Sharpe is in a significant position of command. He's ultimately the one making the decisions, whether or not the Rifles like it. Cornwell presents the trials and tribulations of having command of a group of people in a wartime setting very well, and it's great to watch Sharpe deal with them. This book really feels like we're getting into the meat and potatoes of the series now: Sharpe's experiences in the Napoleonic Wars. The naval voyage, espionage, and small military engagements of the previous books are great stories for the character to shine in but at his heart Sharpe is a soldier above all things, as Cornwell is fond of reminding us.<br /><br />This entry was definitely one of the most entertaining of the series so far, things start out explosive and disorienting and Sharpe and his men struggle violently to survive in war-torn Spain. I loved Blas Vivar, the Spanish Major that Sharpe falls in with early in the book. He's charismatic, stubborn, funny, brave and I enjoyed his stories about his ancestors and the Reconquista. Together they predictably wreak havoc on the French in a series of gripping encounters. When you think Napoleon you usually think big-scale set piece battles but a lot of fighting in the Peninsular War was just mean and nasty guerrilla warfare; really, the perfect setting for Sharpe.<br /><br />All of that good stuff makes this one of my favorite <i>Sharpe</i> books so far. Then the other shoe drops...some of my friends on this site will be completely aware of how irritating I find the love stories in these books. This one was just utterly vapid and pointless. If I remember correctly there was even a scene where Sharpe first meets this girl and the chapter ended with something like "And Richard Sharpe was in love." and I just wanted to fucking hurl the book in disgust. <br /><br />Please understand this is not some macho knee-jerk reaction to the concept of love stories. When well done, they can either add a wonderful level of dimension and emotional reaction from the reader or even stand as fully-formed and satisfying stories in their own stead. The love stories in these books will NEVER accomplish either of those things. I don't get why Cornwell keeps insisting on doing this shit. It literally adds nothing at this point. I liked some of the stuff with Grace but this needs to stop. It would even be more tolerable if Cornwell handled it in a James Bond-type manner, where Sharpe is just basically trying to get laid but he's not. It's almost insulting at this point how Cornwell demands on putting these subplots in every book and it's also a testament to how much I like these books that I'm willing to put up with this bullshit.<br /><br />So yeah...a very fun but clearly flawed entry in the series. I'm obviously gonna read another one of these in like a week or two and see what happens when the war heats up and how Sharpe continues to grow as a commander. It's so hard to describe what makes him such a compelling character; he just utterly lacks pretense and has this vicious, scary desire to live and fight while also managing to somehow be likable and approachable, but can also be cold as ice and has straight up committed murder at this point in the series to further his own goals. It's that kind of uncomfortable but compelling complexity that makes him my favorite Cornwell character.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17828315772679797309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_27331752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27331752" />
    <a title="Melissa McShane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane"><img alt="Melissa McShane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216147749p2/1329059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27331752?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa McShane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa McShane" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane">Melissa McShane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140110142" href="/book/show/1215473.Sharpe_s_Rifles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=napoleonic-wars">napoleonic-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27331752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15452988585418787285">I re-read this as research for the next Extraordinaries book, not for facts so much as Cornwell's gift for capturing the feel of the Spanish countryside and the grittiness of military life in the 19th century. Cornwell's style in this early novel isn't as solid as it becomes in later books, and there's a feeling of distance sometimes between the story and the reader, but that's really my only complaint. Richard Sharpe is a compelling character whose strengths and weaknesses combine to keep him f</span>
  <span id="freeText15452988585418787285" style="display:none">I re-read this as research for the next Extraordinaries book, not for facts so much as Cornwell's gift for capturing the feel of the Spanish countryside and the grittiness of military life in the 19th century. Cornwell's style in this early novel isn't as solid as it becomes in later books, and there's a feeling of distance sometimes between the story and the reader, but that's really my only complaint. Richard Sharpe is a compelling character whose strengths and weaknesses combine to keep him from being absurdly heroic or a grim anti-hero. I like him very much, even when he's being less than heroic, because Cornwell never falters in his characterization.<br /><br />I'm a fan of the movies as well, and what struck me this time was how well I remembered all the secondary characters and how easy it was to picture the actors who played them. The casting choices for Sharpe's men were remarkable.<br /><br />The other thing that struck me was how well Cornwell put together Sharpe's backstory. He wrote the books out of chronological order, which means he had to make things he wrote in this book stay consistent with the stories he wrote later of Sharpe's early career. That is <i>hard</i>, but from what I could tell, Cornwell didn't make any major contradictions. I find that impressive.<br /><br />And the story is just so exciting. Battles, skirmishes, sieges, infiltration under cover of night...it's compelling without being overwhelming. I've docked it a star because of what I said earlier about Cornwell's style, which intruded on my reading experience occasionally, but in general this is just incredibly engaging. I look forward to more re-reading of the other books in the near future. And I think I've nearly convinced myself to make my next heroine a rifleman.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15452988585418787285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11470327988677341380" style="display:none">One of the best books so far in the series. His encounter with Harper is memorable and I look forward to further interaction between the two personalities. <span id="freeTextContainer1146936370457526955">I have really enjoyed this fantastic series by Bernard Cornwell, but this novel is, if not the most intricately plotted, saturated with battle after battle between Sharpe and his Rifles and the pursuing French hordes invading Spain in 1809. Right from the get go, the gore of the skirmishes leaves the reader feeling a bit queasy, and Cornwell rarely lets up. In fact I would say that every second chapter has some form of fight, conflict or attack that leaves the haggard main character, Richard Sha</span>
  <span id="freeText1146936370457526955" style="display:none">I have really enjoyed this fantastic series by Bernard Cornwell, but this novel is, if not the most intricately plotted, saturated with battle after battle between Sharpe and his Rifles and the pursuing French hordes invading Spain in 1809. Right from the get go, the gore of the skirmishes leaves the reader feeling a bit queasy, and Cornwell rarely lets up. In fact I would say that every second chapter has some form of fight, conflict or attack that leaves the haggard main character, Richard Sharpe, just a little more haggard each time. Now, as I said, once the smoke of the last musket blast clears, the plot isn't terribly complex, but war and the period in which this novel takes place wasn't always either; it was governed by the simple concept of "kill or be killed", and Richard Sharpe is one hell of a good soldier. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2032407987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9258497907135718591">My first introduction to Richard Sharpe, soldier & reluctant officer. This is the book that tells how Sharpe ended up as the Leader of his Rifle contingent. I know little of the battles in this era (1809) between Britain & France or Spain & France, so always interesting to me to learn something new. Cornwell definitely paints the gritty picture of difficult marches & bloody battles with the weaponry of the day. I found myself invested in the lives of the main characters, although the character o</span>
  <span id="freeText9258497907135718591" style="display:none">My first introduction to Richard Sharpe, soldier &amp; reluctant officer. This is the book that tells how Sharpe ended up as the Leader of his Rifle contingent. I know little of the battles in this era (1809) between Britain &amp; France or Spain &amp; France, so always interesting to me to learn something new. Cornwell definitely paints the gritty picture of difficult marches &amp; bloody battles with the weaponry of the day. I found myself invested in the lives of the main characters, although the character of Louisa was somewhat unbelievable and was she there just to show a different side of Sharpe. The relationship between Sharpe &amp; Don Blas Vivar was particularly good and helps to evolve Sharpe's character &amp; growth as an officer/leader. The mythos of a sacred &amp; potentially miraculous object gave the story a sense of old fashioned adventure at times amidst the blood &amp; violence.</span>

<div id="review_3089454663" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3089454663" />
    <a title="Lea Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3987635-lea-ann"><img alt="Lea Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475251812p2/3987635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3089454663?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lea Ann" href="/user/show/3987635-lea-ann">Lea Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3089454663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14547364471414489001">Ah okay, I get it. I kind of messed this up. When I was browsing a used book store in Riverside, California for a return flight read (that was my first mistake, travelling cross country with ONE book), I picked up a copy of Sharpe's Rifles that helpfully had a #1 pasted to the spine and the inside sheet placed it as the first book in the Sharpe series. <br/><br/>Listen, I have a love affair with Uhtred in Cornwell's Saxon Stories series. So I was fairly confident he could get me through a return flight. A</span>
  <span id="freeText14547364471414489001" style="display:none">Ah okay, I get it. I kind of messed this up. When I was browsing a used book store in Riverside, California for a return flight read (that was my first mistake, travelling cross country with ONE book), I picked up a copy of Sharpe's Rifles that helpfully had a #1 pasted to the spine and the inside sheet placed it as the first book in the Sharpe series. <br /><br />Listen, I have a love affair with Uhtred in Cornwell's Saxon Stories series. So I was fairly confident he could get me through a return flight. And I'm trying to set up my exit strategy for when I run out of Uhtred books to read. So I felt like I knew what I was doing starting with Sharpe's Rifles. But I could tell something was a little off when I started reading the book.<br /><br />First, I didn't really like Sharpe. He was boorish and not very perceptive. Listen, I get he's supposed to be an amazing soldier. Having served in the Air Force, I know the kinds of attitudes that STILL exist regarding prior enlisted officers, but even so, I mean, they guy showed zero leadership abilities and then could not imagine why he didn't inspire loyalty? <br /><br />Second, some of the secondary characters seemed a little bland. I understand now that Harper is to become a beloved figure in the series, but things do not start out well with the taciturn Irish man who definitely DOES NOT want to be a sergeant. <br /><br />Finally, there is a badly jammed in love triangle that does basically nothing to inspire the plot other than to make Sharpe appear a little more foolish than he already does. How is the guy who can figure NOTHING else out inspire so little confidence and somehow become a beloved literary figure? I wasn't buying it. <br /><br />Thank goodness I found out this book is not the foundation for the series but a prequel. So, question, should I read these in publishing date order, or chronological order? </span>
  <a data-text-id="14547364471414489001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1620140288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1620140288" />
    <a title="Roy Elmer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7164326-roy-elmer"><img alt="Roy Elmer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339248993p2/7164326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1620140288?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Elmer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Elmer" href="/user/show/7164326-roy-elmer">Roy Elmer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007425860" href="/book/show/12315990-sharpe-s-rifles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7164326-roy-elmer?shelf=my-favourite-books">my-favourite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7164326-roy-elmer?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7164326-roy-elmer?shelf=favourite-series">favourite-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7164326-roy-elmer?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7164326-roy-elmer?shelf=sharpe">sharpe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1620140288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11114426304568900801">So, this is the sixth Sharpe book I've read so far, and quite a brief span of time. I do this from time to time. I'll pick up a series and read until the fatigue sets in. Discworld is the most recent example. The trouble with this approach is that it results in needing to take a breather and as the author's style and tropes become so familiar, it breeds a sort of complacent dislike for the material. Not because it isn't good, but because the turns of phrase can be repeated, or in Pratchett's cas</span>
  <span id="freeText11114426304568900801" style="display:none">So, this is the sixth Sharpe book I've read so far, and quite a brief span of time. I do this from time to time. I'll pick up a series and read until the fatigue sets in. Discworld is the most recent example. The trouble with this approach is that it results in needing to take a breather and as the author's style and tropes become so familiar, it breeds a sort of complacent dislike for the material. Not because it isn't good, but because the turns of phrase can be repeated, or in Pratchett's case, the same jokes pop up. I'm writing this for context really, as in the case of Cornwell's Sharpe books, this just isn't happening, despite my fear that it might after Sharpe's Prey. In fact, Cornwell has managed to do something really difficult in that he's got the balance just right between consistency, character growth and ensuring that a series doesn't stagnate. I would go as far as to say that Sharpe's Rifles is my favourite entry yet.<br /><br />We do see the familiar pattern. Sharpe is questioning his worth. Again. This isn't about whether he belongs in the army at all, it's more about whether he can be a leader of men. This is progression of a sort, and it helps us to understand Sharpe's desire to (to coin a phrase) be all that he can be; a rogue, but a fundamentally good man. His struggles form the backbone of this novel, and it's fascinating to follow the progress of the man's career in its own right. That's not the only thing that this book, and this series has going for it, however.<br /><br />The Sharpe novels are quite short. Historical authors have a tendency to produce something huge and stuffed full of detail, to the point of becoming a wee bit bloated in places and a bit of a trudge to get to the plot progression. Pillars of the Earth, I'm looking at you. Cornwell doesn't do this. He somehow manages to build the historical detail in to 330 or so pages, without it becoming overwhelming, or bloated, or dull, but at the same time giving his reader enough to feel immersed in the period, and, perhaps importantly of all, to understand Sharpe's motives.<br /><br />We know Sharpe is going to come out of whatever scrape he's in in one piece, and he will, chances are, be a hero. This is why you pay the price of admission, so don't be surprised by that. The Sharpe novels are dangerous books for boys, written for adults. The pace is fast, whilst retaining the detail mentioned above, and this is such a difficult balancing act to achieve. Cornwell needs credit for that. He is a historian, but he wears it lightly.<br /><br />One of my quibbles with the Sharpe novels is the potential for the portrayal of women as trophies, or swooning airheads. It happened a bit in the opening Indian novels, Sharpe's Trafalgar perhaps less so and in Prey it diminished further. In Rifles we see two women, and two women only. One is a caricature of a battleaxe, a sort of necessary plot device, lacking in detail, the other is strong and youthful, with a mind of her own and the free will and agency with which to turn Sharpe down. It adds an extra, and welcome dimension to these novels that the gender balance is not so skewed, and if anything it creates a touch more humanity behind the guns and powder smoke.<br /><br />I've given this a five, as after the lull of the previous couple of entries, it feels as if the series is back on track. The war in Spain makes sense in a historical context, Sharpe's joining up with a Spanish cavalry battalion is perhaps not entirely unbelievable, and the fact that we really get to see him test his mettle as a leader of men make this the best entry so far. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11114426304568900801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40693649" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40693649" />
    <a title="Linda Banche" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1521544-linda-banche"><img alt="Linda Banche" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485557555p2/1521544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40693649?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Banche" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Banche" href="/user/show/1521544-linda-banche">Linda Banche</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40693649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4371378745420120430">Set during the Napoleonic Wars, Sharpe's Rifles is the story of soldier Richard Sharpe. War stories, full as they are of blood and guts, are not exactly my cup of tea. But Sharpe's Rifles is also a rousing adventure story, with plot twists that take you straight to the end, loads of descriptions, and even a bit of romance. Highly researched, the book is chock-full of historical detail, which I love, since it puts you right into the period.<br/><br/>But the best part is the self-doubting Sharpe, soldier ex</span>
  <span id="freeText4371378745420120430" style="display:none">Set during the Napoleonic Wars, Sharpe's Rifles is the story of soldier Richard Sharpe. War stories, full as they are of blood and guts, are not exactly my cup of tea. But Sharpe's Rifles is also a rousing adventure story, with plot twists that take you straight to the end, loads of descriptions, and even a bit of romance. Highly researched, the book is chock-full of historical detail, which I love, since it puts you right into the period.<br /><br />But the best part is the self-doubting Sharpe, soldier extraordinaire, and a man who's pulled himself up by his bootstraps at a time it was almost impossible to do so. Although a hardened soldier, Sharpe is also very appealing. Promoted from the ranks, he questions his ability to function as an officer, but he never lets his doubts interfere with his job, although he is tempted to let them. <br /><br />Sharpe's Rifles is part of a series, so I have a lot more books to read to find out what happens to him.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4371378745420120430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9333182627875883355" style="display:none">Adore the new covers (even if they don't match the rest of my Sharpe books) and found Sharpe's Rifles to be the same enjoyable Sharpe tale I've come to love. I couldn't put it down, but did feel that Sharpe was quite a bit more surly and churlish in this novel, but with the similar brashness as before. <br /><br />Never quite trusted the love interest either, but I've come to learn that where Sharpe and women are concerned, it rarely ends well. Harper is already fantastic and my favourite part of the book is a conversation between Harper and Hagman, concerning the posh back-stabbing bint:<br /><br />“She’s got more sense than to marry him. He ain’t ready for a chain and shackle, is he? She needs someone a bit steady, she does.”<br />“But he was sotted on her.”<br />“He would be, wouldn’t he? He’ll fall in love with anything in a petticoat. I’ve seen his type before. Got the sense of a half-witted sheep when it comes to women.” Harper spat. “It’s a good job he’s got me to look after him now.”</span>

<div id="review_1771893078" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1771893078" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1771893078?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin Webster" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin Webster" href="/user/show/7548247-robin-webster">Robin Webster</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1771893078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9856068769968993640">‘Sharpe’s Rifles’ is centred on the mainly fictional account of events in 1809 after a small expeditionary army sent to Spain in 1809 to support the Spanish and to cut off the invading French Army's supply lines. The story opens when the British army is forced to retreat and Lieutenant Sharpe finds himself for the first time in charge of a unit of his Rifle regiment. They find themselves cut off from the main army and the soldiers are reluctant to take orders from someone of their own class. Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText9856068769968993640" style="display:none">‘Sharpe’s Rifles’ is centred on the mainly fictional account of events in 1809 after a small expeditionary army sent to Spain in 1809 to support the Spanish and to cut off the invading French Army's supply lines. The story opens when the British army is forced to retreat and Lieutenant Sharpe finds himself for the first time in charge of a unit of his Rifle regiment. They find themselves cut off from the main army and the soldiers are reluctant to take orders from someone of their own class. This was the first ‘prequel in the Sharpe series, and in a sense it does show, as there is no reference to his relationships he had in the previous book. Also, the book does lean more to fiction than many other books in the series. However, this is a fine entertaining novel, that easily captures the atmosphere of the previous books and I’m sure it won’t be long before I’ll be tucking in to the next book in the series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9856068769968993640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15644591788283152899">I watched the mini series first, and the book is way more intense and I wasn't even sure I liked Sharpe at all 1/2 way through the book. But that's character development for you. The author is a serious history buff and it's clear he knows what he's talking about. Some of it you kind of gloss over (which battalion was flanking which... come on!) but mostly he manages to sneak in interesting little facts like the English didn't smoke cigarettes, they only had little clay pipes. There's plenty of </span>
  <span id="freeText15644591788283152899" style="display:none">I watched the mini series first, and the book is way more intense and I wasn't even sure I liked Sharpe at all 1/2 way through the book. But that's character development for you. The author is a serious history buff and it's clear he knows what he's talking about. Some of it you kind of gloss over (which battalion was flanking which... come on!) but mostly he manages to sneak in interesting little facts like the English didn't smoke cigarettes, they only had little clay pipes. There's plenty of swashbuckling just like the mini series, but the books are a little more swash and a little less buckle if you get my drift. <br /><br />Merged review:<br /><br />Yikes! The tv series begins with this story, and the differences couldn't be more striking. The tv series really softened up the characters and the situation. That being said, the book (predicatably) was richer and more complex.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/519922411" />
    <a title="Marc Haddock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15480573-marc-haddock"><img alt="Marc Haddock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371242869p2/15480573.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/519922411?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc Haddock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc Haddock" href="/user/show/15480573-marc-haddock">Marc Haddock</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer519922411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4564918351500960757">I first met Richard Cornwell's books when I read his Arthurian series, and I've yet to read one of his books I didn't enjoy. I am working my way through the Sharpe series in chronological order and have enjoyed every one.<br/><br/>This book was written late in the series to provide an introduction to Sharpe and his Rifles, mainly to explain the relationship Sharpe forges here with his men and his brilliant Irish sergeant. It is great fun. The battle scenes are written with clarity and a great feel for act</span>
  <span id="freeText4564918351500960757" style="display:none">I first met Richard Cornwell's books when I read his Arthurian series, and I've yet to read one of his books I didn't enjoy. I am working my way through the Sharpe series in chronological order and have enjoyed every one.<br /><br />This book was written late in the series to provide an introduction to Sharpe and his Rifles, mainly to explain the relationship Sharpe forges here with his men and his brilliant Irish sergeant. It is great fun. The battle scenes are written with clarity and a great feel for action. Cornwell writes the best battle scenes from a variety of time periods -- from the shield wall of medieval England to the Napoleonic wars.<br /><br />I look forward to reading Sharpe's Havoc, the next in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4564918351500960757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer627415067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17101757495582874117">The story that starts Sharpe's campaign on the mainland peninsula, although of course by this time he's fought in India, gained a commission for saving Wellington's life, and also helped out at Trafalgar. During that time he's become fabulously wealthy and then reduced to pauper again. Here he learns his craft as an officer, how to lead his company of riflemen who first want to kill him, and then grow to follow him. It's the start of the friendship between Sharpe and Harper. Sharpe gets a few hi</span>
  <span id="freeText17101757495582874117" style="display:none">The story that starts Sharpe's campaign on the mainland peninsula, although of course by this time he's fought in India, gained a commission for saving Wellington's life, and also helped out at Trafalgar. During that time he's become fabulously wealthy and then reduced to pauper again. Here he learns his craft as an officer, how to lead his company of riflemen who first want to kill him, and then grow to follow him. It's the start of the friendship between Sharpe and Harper. Sharpe gets a few hints on officership by Vivar, his Spanish friend, there's a huge(ish) battle at the end which Sharpe seemingly can't win, and of course, a beautiful woman. The writing isn't the best, a few annoying text repetitions, but what the hell, Bernard Cornwell has sold a lot more books than I ever will.</span>

<div id="review_1597375151" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1597375151" />
    <a title="Sam Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47470600-sam-taylor"><img alt="Sam Taylor" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1597375151?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Taylor" href="/user/show/47470600-sam-taylor">Sam Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1597375151&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1597375151" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5261193313313455113">This book is a very fast read that you could finish in a day or two. The story is action packed and takes a turn when you are least expecting it. Sharpe and Harper's relationship development throughout the story is full of bumps along the road. Yet it flowers into a solid friendship that they both benefit from. This story follows how sharp being thrown into a uncomfortable position manages to make it work and earn the respect from his fellow soldiers.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<li class='cover' id='bookCover_2203222'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2203222.Midshipman_Bolitho_and_the_Avenger"><img alt="Midshipman Bolitho and the Avenger (Richard Bolitho, #2)" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1258887642l/2203222._SX98_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nuxHXAcgeg1SZH9RcEQRDvR6VPra8xVbYHSdTccHQHv59THDJXGkAXSQQSBe8PFao4CyPQqCIk0mLDLuXMMMJA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1504578807p3/12542.jpg);'></div>
